Source: 2026-06-02 The Past and Present of Stream Processing (Part 21): The RisingWave | by Gang Tao | Medium

WebSync metadata title: The Past and Present of Stream Processing (Part 21): The RisingWave | by Gang Tao | Medium url: https://taogang.medium.com/the-past-and-present-of-stream-processing-part-21-the-risingwave-7b59dbf82fbd date: 2026-06-02T19:41:00.648Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap First published in April 2022, RisingWave is a distributed, Rust-written SQL streaming database that claims to achieve 10x cost efficiency compared to Apache Flink while maintaining higher performance. RisingWave has rapidly evolved from an ambitious new project to a production system used by over 1,000 organizations. The version 2.6 released in September 2025 introduced vector search and AI capabilities. Its core innovation lies in combining PostgreSQL protocol compatibility with a cloud-native decoupled architecture, unifying stream computing, persistent storage, and query services within a single system, eliminating the traditional need for separate stream processing engines, state storage, and serving databases. Existing stream processing solutions often face a difficult choice: either complex and expensive enterprise-level systems requiring specialized knowledge (like Apache Flink), or lightweight tools with limited SQL capabilities (like ksqlDB). RisingWave fills this gap by making stream processing more accessible through standard SQL, while demonstrating significant advantages in benchmark-validated performance. The platform outperforms Flink in 22 of 27 Nexmark queries, with some queries achieving 660x speedup, and can recover from failures in seconds (rather than minutes), while supporting elastic scaling without service interruption. RisingWave was founded in early 2021 by Wu Yingjun (former AWS Redshift engineer with ten years of database system experience, who also frequently publishes articles on Zhihu), and completed a $36 million Series A funding round in October 2022. The technical evolution timeline is: early 2021 development began → April 2022 open-sourced under Apache 2.0 license → July 2023 released production-ready version 1.0 → September 2024 released version 2.0, entering the stream-batch unification era. As of October 2025, the project has garnered 8,400+ GitHub stars, 136+ active contributors, and 2,000+ Slack community members, marking its growth from a promising startup project to a mature open-source database project. Developer Experience Like Materialize, RisingWave adopts fully compatible PostgreSQL protocol. Therefore, it can seamlessly connect using standard connection strings (such as postgresql://user:password@host:port/database). It transparently supports psql, JDBC drivers, pgcli, SQLAlchemy, and almost all PostgreSQL ecosystem tools, from DBeaver and DataGrip to Metabase and Superset. RisingWave extends SQL for stream processing-related functionality. For example, creating a data source from Kafka demonstrates its declarative style: For time windows, RisingWave abstracts tumbling, hopping, and session windows as SQL functions, without requiring complex programming logic. For example, a fraud detection SQL example using tumbling windows: In RisingWave, materialized views are the core abstraction, representing continuous queries that incrementally update as new data arrives. For example, creating a sink to write results back to Kafka can complete end-to-end pipeline definition in pure SQL: In time-varying relations, tables continuously change over time: Time-varying relations are represented by change streams, where each record contains: Operation type: INSERT, DELETE, UPDATE_DELETE (before update), UPDATE_INSERT (after update) Data: Specific row content Timestamp: When the change occurred This model allows RisingWave to use SQL for stream data processing: The system automatically incrementally updates results for each change: INSERT (001, 100) → total = 100 INSERT (002, 200) → total = 300 UPDATE (001, 150) → total = 350 (+50) DELETE (002) → total = 150 (-200) The Time-Varying Relations model is the bridge connecting “database thinking” and “stream processing.” Comparison with Materialize RisingWave and Materialize are very similar in many aspects. Let’s compare these two products. Both are positioned as streaming databases. Similarities include: Both are streaming databases Both implemented in Rust Both support PostgreSQL protocol Both use materialized views as core abstraction But key differences reveal their strategic divergence: Architecture: Materialize initially single-node, later expanded to distributed; RisingWave was distributed from the start License: Materialize uses BSL (Business Source License) with usage restrictions; RisingWave uses Apache 2.0, completely open source without usage restrictions Engine: Materialize built on Timely Dataflow; RisingWave uses its self-developed LSM-tree storage engine Hummock Focus: Materialize emphasizes strong consistency and operational intelligence; RisingWave emphasizes democratizing stream processing and cost efficiency In design philosophy, fundamental differences exist: Materialize takes a bottom-up approach (Timely Dataflow → SQL engine) RisingWave takes a top-down approach (starting from SQL natively) Summary As a representative of streaming databases, RisingWave has outstanding advantages: Based on the previous in-depth analysis, RisingWave’s core strengths can be summarized as: Low Learning Threshold: Fully compatible with PostgreSQL syntax, if you know SQL you can use it From zero to first stream processing pipeline within 30 minutes No need to learn complex APIs (compared to Flink requiring weeks) Significant Cost Efficiency: Cloud-native architecture with separated compute and storage Using S3 for state storage costs 10x less than local disks Claims total cost 2–15x lower than Flink Simple Operations: Second-level dynamic scaling (Flink requires minutes to hours) Second-level failure recovery (Flink requires minutes to hours) Single system replaces multi-component architecture (no need for Kafka + Flink + Redis) Friendly Open Source License: Apache 2.0 license, no usage restrictions However, RisingWave also has certain limitations and various issues, including: Operational Challenges (from documented GitHub issues): When Hummock storage files are missing, recovery process can hang (404 errors cause infinite retry loops) LocalStack S3 integration requires careful path-style access configuration MinIO integration has challenges detecting new files Temporary storage failures may lead to unrecoverable state, requiring manual intervention → These issues indicate the platform is still in a “hardening” phase for operational robustness Performance Weaknesses (in specific scenarios): When compute node memory is limited and access patterns are irregular, high cache miss rates significantly impact performance Heavy aggregation computations using functions like array_agg are costly Joins on low-cardinality columns (very few distinct values) easily trigger massive row amplification When local cache is insufficient, frequent remote storage access slows performance When downstream operators process slower than upstream, backpressure occurs, slowing down the entire pipeline Enterprise Gaps: Architecture: Recent versions (v2.1) switched metadata storage from etcd to PostgreSQL, reflecting architectural “growing pains” in scalability Documentation: Production documentation still being improved, migration paths not mature Service Ecosystem: Professional service ecosystem weak, far fewer consultants and integrators available compared to Flink Recently, Wu Yingjun posted on LinkedIn showing the company has very strong growth momentum. If this data is accurate, congratulations to RisingWave and his team — well done! Thanks for reading! I’m the co-founder and CTO of Timeplus. Proton https://github.com/timeplus-io/proton is our open-source, high-performance streaming SQL engine built in C++. It enables you to process real-time data streams using standard SQL, perfect for real-time security and monitoring, real-time machine learning pipelines, IoT analytics, and more. Feel free to star us on GitHub or join our Slack community at https://timeplus.com/slack   to discuss the future of streaming data together! WebSync metadata title: Real-Time Data Validation using Great Expectations | by Vishal Periyasamy Rajendran | Medium url: https://medium.com/@vishalrv1904/real-time-data-validation-using-great-expectations-27707b6b0404 date: 2026-06-02T19:40:47.612Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Introduction: Data validation is the process of ensuring data has undergone data cleansing to make sure it has data quality, which means that it’s both correct and useful. Data validation uses routines, often called “validation rules,” “validation constraints,” or “check routines.” They check for the correctness, meaningfulness, and security of the data that is input into the system. There are many types of data validation, but the most common are: Type validation — used to check whether data is of a given type, for instance, that a number is an int, not a float; Range and constraint validation —  e.g. human height is smaller than 300 cm and greater than 0 because historically we can tell that the tallest person who has ever lived was shorter than 300 cm; Code and cross-reference validation  — A given value is in [a,b,c]; Structured validation —  (more complex validation); Consistency validation —  To check, for example, whether the date of sales happens before the date of shipping. Data validation became crucial as more and more companies and organizations rely upon bigger and more complex data sets, collected from different sources to draw insights and make crucial business decisions. Great Expectations: Great Expectation is a useful tool to profile, validate, and document data. It helps to maintain the quality of data throughout a data workflow and pipeline. Great Expectations Key features of Great Expectations: Expectations are like assertions in traditional Python unit tests. Automated data profiling automates pipeline tests. Data Contexts and Data Sources allow you to configure connections to your data sources. Tooling for validation is the checkpoint for data validation. Data Docs are clean, human-readable documentation. Scenario: An application sends events to the data collection layer. The collection layer stores the source of truth on the raw zone and pushes the data into a Kinesis stream. Validation lambda checks the sanity of the collected data using the rules stored on the S3 bucket. If the condition passes it will be processed & stored by the Consumer. Else the DynamoDb tracker is updated with the error details. High-Level Architecture Diagram: Creating Validation Rules: Install the great expectation using pip. Create configuration rules based on the below format. Update the suite config and execute the code. Generated validation suite will look like this, Create multiple validation suites based on the use case and store them on the S3 bucket. Validation Layer: Python Lambda Code: validation.py Docker lambda code: Overall Architecture Diagram: Conclusion: The data validation process is an important step in data and analytics workflows to filter quality data and improve the efficiency of the overall process. It not only produces data that is reliable, consistent, and accurate but also makes data handling easier. Working with correct and valid data is an important criterion for the success of any data solution project. Great Expectation is a great tool that can help save time and promote analytic integrity for your data pipelines. References: https://greatexpectations.io/ https://www.stxnext.com/blog/data-validation-big-data-environment/ https://medium.com/hashmapinc/understanding-great-expectations-and-how-to-use-it-7754c78962f4 Senior Data Engineer at Presidio — Certified cloud professional with specialisation in architecting & developing big data solutions at AWS cloud.

Source: 2026-06-02 Building a Semantic Layer from Scratch on an existing dbt project. | by Eva Mwangi | Apr, 2026 | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Building a Semantic Layer from Scratch on an existing dbt project. | by Eva Mwangi | Apr, 2026 | Medium url: https://medium.com/@evaGachirwa/building-a-semantic-layer-from-scratch-on-an-existing-dbt-project-54fac86748cc date: 2026-06-02T19:40:33.795Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap A practical guide to MetricFlow — the errors, the fixes, and the lessons learned We recently added a semantic layer to our dbt project using MetricFlow. On paper, it looked straightforward. In practice, we hit a series of errors that weren’t well documented anywhere in one place. This post covers the full journey — what the semantic layer is, how we built it, every error we encountered, and how we fixed each one. What is the dbt Semantic Layer? Before MetricFlow, every analyst and every BI tool defined metrics independently. “Weekly revenue” meant something slightly different in Looker, in a Jupyter notebook, and in a dbt model. The semantic layer solves this by giving metrics a single authoritative definition that any tool can query. The stack looks like this: The key distinction most tutorials gloss over:
Measures are raw aggregation building blocks (sum, count, average). They live in the semantic model. End users never query them directly.
Metrics are business definitions built on top of measures. They live in a separate metrics file. This is what analysts and BI tools query. A metric cannot exist without a measure. A measure cannot exist without a semantic model. The chain must be complete. — - The Files We Created We structured our semantic layer in a dedicated folder separate from models/:
The Semantic Model The semantic model maps a mart’s columns into entities, dimensions, and measures: The Metrics File Metrics reference measures by name. Simple metrics wrap a single measure. Derived metrics combine multiple metrics with an expression: One lesson here: don’t use pre-calculated percentage columns from your mart as measures. If your mart has a sales_wow_pct column pre-calculated via a LAG function, summing it across rows gives you a sum of percentages — which is meaningless. Instead, define the component measures (sales_wow_change, last_week_sales) and re-derive the percentage at the metric layer. The Global Namespace Problem This is the most important thing to understand about MetricFlow that the docs bury. DBT models are scoped — a model called orders in models/online_sales/ and a model called orders in models/break_and_mortar_sales/ can coexist because they resolve to different schemas. The semantic layer has no such scoping. Every entity, dimension, measure, and metric name is registered in a single global namespace across your entire project. This means if you define a total_sales metric for one domain and then build a second domain that also needs total_sales, you get a parse error. The fix: prefix every name with the domain. E.g., of globally unique This applies to everything: entity names, dimension names, measure names, and metric names. All of them share the same namespace. Document this convention in a README at the root of your semantic models folder so new team members don’t learn it the hard way. Errors We Hit (and How We Fixed Them)
metric-paths and semantic-model-paths are not valid keys Additional properties are not allowed (‘metric-paths’, ‘semantic-model-paths’ were unexpected) What happened: We added separate path config keys to dbt_project.yml based on outdated documentation. dbt 1.6+ doesn’t have these keys. Fix: Add your semantic models folder to model-paths instead — dbt discovers semantic model and metrics YAML files from the same paths as regular models:
MetricFlow requires a time spine The semantic layer requires a time spine model with granularity DAY or smaller What happened: MetricFlow needs a table with one row per day to perform time-based calculations — truncating to week/month/year, filling date gaps, and resolving period comparisons. It doesn’t generate this itself. Fix: Create a time spine model. If you have dbt_utils installed: With the corresponding YAML to tell MetricFlow this is the time spine: The start date should align with the earliest date in your data.
meta: is not allowed on semantic models Additional properties are not allowed (‘meta’ was unexpected) What happened: We tried to add a meta: block to the semantic model for documentation purposes. dbt 1.10 does not support meta: on semantic model definitions. Fix: Move context into YAML comments at the top of the file instead:
Testing locally with the MetricFlow CLI dbt sl commands only works in dbt Cloud. For local testing, use the mf CLI that ships with dbt-metricflow. Setup: Testing workflow: Key Takeaways
Measures ≠ metrics. Measures are private building blocks. Metrics are the public interface. Both are required.
Prefix everything with your domain name. MetricFlow has one global namespace. No exceptions.
Always add expr to dimensions. Don’t rely on implicit column name matching when your dimension names differ from column names.
Don’t sum pre-calculated percentages. Re-derive them at the metric layer from their components.
Time spine is not optional. Any project with time dimensions needs one.
Pin dbt-core and dbt-adapter to the same minor version. Upgrade them together.
dbt parse first, always. MetricFlow validation won’t run if the project doesn’t parse. This is the first time I am using semantic layers. What trade-offs or errors surprised you? Hey, I am a data engineer sharing hard-won lessons from building pipelines at scale. Follow for practical insights on modern data stacks and real solutions.

Source: 2026-06-02 End-to-End Exactly Once Processing in Flink with Non-Transactional Data Stores | by Bijeet Singh | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: End-to-End Exactly Once Processing in Flink with Non-Transactional Data Stores | by Bijeet Singh | Medium url: https://medium.com/@bijeetsingh/end-to-end-exactly-once-processing-in-apache-flink-with-non-transactional-data-stores-af057b563250 date: 2026-06-02T19:40:20.946Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Introduction End-to-end exactly-once processing is a guarantee provided by a stream processing framework to ensure that data is processed exactly once across the entire pipeline, from ingestion (data sources) through processing to output (data sinks). Specifically, it ensures the following: No duplication in the result: Each event is processed only once, and the final result, including data written to external sinks, reflects this guarantee. Even in the event of failures (e.g., system crashes or restarts), the system ensures that no messages are lost or processed more than once. Consistency across sources and sinks: Exactly-once semantics apply not only to the processing of data within the framework but also to interactions with external systems (such as message queues, databases, or file systems). This means that no duplicate records are written to external sinks and that stateful operations (like aggregations or joins) are consistent even in the face of failures. Flink supports end-to-end exactly once processing using the Two-Phase Commit algorithm. This requires the external data store where we write data from Flink, to support transactions. In this post, we will discuss the design for achieving end-to-end exactly once processing with data stores that do not support transaction. Along the way, we will also cover transactions in general, Flink checkpoints, and transactions in the context of stream processing in Flink. Recoverability in Flink Checkpoints Flink provides mechanisms for job recovery in case of failure, with checkpoints being at the heart of this process. Operators are the building blocks of a Flink application and they typically maintain an inherent state. For example the state of a Kafka source operator includes the current offset, indicating the position from which the next record should be read, the state of a sink operator might include the buffered records waiting to be written to the external data store, and the window processing operator contains the state of aggregations corresponding to the active windows. Checkpointing is the fault tolerance mechanism in Flink, that periodically captures the state of different operators in a Flink job and allows the job to recover from failure by restoring it’s state using the checkpointed state. Figure 1 illustrates a Flink job that reads from a Kafka topic, performs a tumbling window aggregation, and writes to an external data store. The job comprises of 3 different operators. Checkpoint C1 represents the most recent consistent state of the job, capturing the state of all three operators at a specific point in time. The captured state of each of the three operators represent the following: Source operator : C1 indicates that the source operator has processed events from the Kafka topic up to offset say, P1. Window processing operator : C1 reflects the aggregated state of the active windows until the time it has processed the event corresponding to offset P1 Sink operator : for sink operator, C1 represents any buffered record resulting from the upstream processing of events until offset P1 If the job fails and recovers by restoring it’s state from checkpoint C1, the restored state of this job represents the point in time until when all the operators of this job had processed events up to offset P1. Is Checkpointing Sufficient for End-to-End Exactly Once Processing? Recovering from a checkpoint does not guarantee end-to-end exactly-once processing. While it may work in certain scenarios, it does not suffice for providing end-to-end exactly once processing in all cases. Let’s look at a scenario represented by Figure 2 below: Things to note about this figure: It represents a Flink job that reads from a Kafka topic, performs a tumbling window aggregation and writes the result to InfluxDB. Let’s assume the job uses processing-timefor creating the tumbling windows. C1 is the last successful checkpoint. It corresponds to the events processed from the Kafka topic up to offset P1 and T1 was the system time when event at P1 was consumed. The next checkpoint, C2 is expected to happen at a future time T. Since the last successful checkpoint C1, the job has processed events until offset P2 where P2 > P1, corresponding to time T2, where T1 < T2 < T. The tumbling window fires at T2 and this means that the aggregated result of the current window, ending at P2 gets written into InfluxDB. Let’s say, at this point, some failure leads to an application restart. When it recovers, it has it’s state restored from the last successful checkpoint C1. This means it has the state corresponding to the consumption of event with offset P1, and it will start consuming events from the Kafka topic after offset P1, this includes the events between offset P1-P2, that were processed earlier as well. When the next window fires it will write the aggregated result to InfluxDB. So, if the writes to InfluxDB are idempotent, the multiple writes to InfluxDB because of this window firing — one before and one after the application restart — should not create duplicate entries, isn’t it? Let’s explore the possibilities in the next section. What about Idempotent Writes to Sink? Well, the answer is it depends, whether or not this leads to duplicate event processing. If the result of this window aggregation is deterministic, both the window fires, before and after the restart, would produce the same result and idempotency on write should take care of the duplicate writes. But, if the window boundaries are itself non-deterministic, the aggregation result is not expected to be deterministic either. So, even when we have provisions for idempotency on write, we are effectively creating different entries in InfluxDB across the two window fires and this leads to some Kafka events accounted for twice in the aggregated result in InfluxDB. Let’s look at Figure 3 for understanding how this scenario plays out: Before the application restart, the last window that fired, had consumed events until offset P2. Let’s say by the time the application restarted, some additional events were pushed in the Kafka topic until offset P3. Now, after the restart, based on the checkpointed state that it has recovered from, it will start the window boundary at the same position as the window that fired before the application restart. But, the end boundary of this window will now be at P3 instead of P2 — since it has additional events this time that can be consumed before the window fires. What this means is, even though the starting boundary of the window is the same, the end boundaries are different and this leads to two different records (aggregated result) being produced on each window fire, instead of two exactly same records being produced. So, even if we have provisions for idempotency, we are effectively producing different records each time and this leads to events from the start of the window boundary until P2 accounted for twice in the InfluxDB across two different points. Clearly, even with provisions for idempotency, we are not able to achieve end-to-end exactly once processing in the scenario covered above. Across the pipeline, we broadly have 2 states: state of the flink job — this comprises of the states of the operators aggregated state maintained in the sink — InfluxDB. The problem that we are facing here is because these two states do not remain in a consistent state in case of recovery from failure. So, what we need here is some kind of atomic commitment algorithm which writes to the different states maintained in the pipeline in an atomic manner. Let’s explore more in the next section. Two-Phase Commit in a Flink Pipeline Two-phase commit is a class of distributed atomic commitment algorithms that ensures a set of operations, bundled in a transaction and resulting in a state change across distributed systems, are either fully committed or fully aborted, even in the presence of failures. Flink provides end-to-end exactly once processing in a pipeline by combining checkpoints with a sink that extend the TwoPhaseCommitSinkFunction class. Looking back at Figure 3, we faced the problem of some records being accounted for twice in the end result in InfluxDB. This was because, between the time the last successful checkpoint happened and when the job failed, some additional events were processed that updated the state in InfluxDB. And after the restart, the state was restored from last checkpoint and the events after that are processed again, resulting in another update in InfluxDB, corresponding to the same records processed earlier. What if, we can ensure that the updates to the final state in InfluxDB are aligned with the checkpoints? This means when checkpoint C1 completes, we update the final state of InfluxDB. The next update in InfluxDB would happen when checkpoint C2 completes. This way, even if there is some failure between C1 and C2, the job would recover from C1 and it will not lead to any duplication in result because no updates have yet gone into InfluxDB. This is exactly what Flink does with the TwoPhaseCommitting sinks — the updates to the sink are bundled in a transaction that exists between the two checkpoints, i.e when C1 completes, a new transaction is started and this transaction is committed when C2 completes successfully. Figure 4 below represents this: State A represents the distributed state (state of source i.e Kafka offsets, Flink operators and the sink i.e InfluxDB) of the end-to-end pipeline, corresponding to the checkpoint C1 and State B represents the state corresponding to the checkpoint C2, when the transaction between C1→C2 completes. There is no intermediate state. More about how end-to-end exactly once processing works and the importance of checkpoints in achieving this can be read here. A distributed transaction relies on the local transactions at each participating system where the state is maintained. If any local transaction fails, the entire distributed transaction fails. This means the support for transactions at each participating system level is essential for distributed transactions to work. But how do we achieve this with data stores used as sink that do not support transaction — InfluxDB for example? Two-Phase Commit with Non-Transactional Data Stores Transaction support in the data store is essential for achieving end-to-end exactly once processing in Flink. For data stores like InfluxDB that do not natively support transactions, we mimic the transaction behaviour to emulate the transaction guarantees. Below are the transaction guarantees that we need to mimic: The result of a transaction is visible only when transaction is complete. Either all the results of the transaction are visible, or none of them are. In case of failure, the result of the transaction should be rolled back Mimicking the transactional behaviour To mimic the three transaction behaviours outlined above, we use an intermediate InfluxDB measurement to stage updates during the prepare phase of the transaction. When the transaction completes during the commit phase, we move the corresponding records of this transaction from the staging measurement to the main measurement. This process involves the following steps: Transaction Initialisation: When a new transaction begins after the completion of a checkpoint, we create a new transaction object with a unique transactionID. Staging Updates: All updates within the transaction are written to the staging measurement in InfluxDB, along with the transactionID. While the transaction is in progress, the results are not written to the main measurement, so they are not visible to end users. For example (as shown in Figure 5), after checkpoint C1 completes, Transaction1 begins, and updates are written to the staging measurement. Multiple records can be written to the staging measurement under the same transaction, especially if there are multiple window fires during the transaction duration. Each record will carry the same transactionID. Transaction Abort During Pre-Commit: If the transaction is aborted at this stage, no updates corresponding to this transaction have been made to the main measurement, so no changes are visible to the end users. Transaction Commit: When the transaction commits, following the completion of checkpoint C2 (as shown in Figure 6), all participants have agreed to commit the transaction. As per the 2PC protocol, there is no going back from here. At this point, we move all records corresponding to this transaction from the staging measurement to the main measurement. This ensures that the transaction’s results are only visible to the end user once it is complete. Transaction Failure During Commit: If a failure occurs while moving data from the staging measurement to the main measurement during the commit phase, Flink will restart the job, recover the last transaction, and retry the operation. This is an idempotent write so the multiple retries will not cause duplication. Flink expects the retry to eventually succeed. However, if the number of configured application restarts is exhausted and the data movement still hasn’t succeeded, this will result in data loss. By following this approach, we effectively mimic the behaviour of a transaction and ensure that the three transaction guarantees mentioned above are upheld. Conclusion In this post, we explored several approaches for achieving end-to-end exactly-once processing in a Flink pipeline. We began with a solution that combines Flink checkpoints with idempotent writes to the sink. While this method works well when the results being written are deterministic, it fails in scenarios where reprocessing occurs in the presence of failures and the results are non-deterministic. Next, we examined Two-Phase Commit (2PC), a class of atomic commitment algorithms, and discussed the importance of transaction support in the data store used as the sink. In cases where the data store does not natively support transactions, we outlined an approach to mimic the transaction behaviour. Although we used InfluxDB as an example, this approach can be applied to other data stores as well to achieve similar transaction guarantees. Software Engineer | Distributed Systems | Graph Databases

Build and centralize metrics with the dbt Semantic Layer | dbt Labs

🎤 Busta Rhymes is performing at dbt Mainstage · June 3, SF · Request access → Big news! 🎉 Fivetran and dbt Labs have merged to deliver the data infrastructure for agents you trust 🎤 Busta Rhymes is performing at dbt Mainstage · June 3, SF · Request access → Big news! 🎉 Fivetran and dbt Labs have merged to deliver the data infrastructure for agents you trust Back Close menu Open menu Product Solutions Customers Resources Community Pricing Get started Book a demo Product Solutions Customers Resources Community Pricing Login Create a free account Get started Book a demo Blog / Product / Build, centralize, and deliver consistent metrics with the dbt Semantic Layer Build, centralize, and deliver consistent metrics with the dbt Semantic Layer Roxi Pourzand last updated on Sep 11, 2024 At Coalesce 2023, we announced the general availability of the revamped dbt Semantic Layer. The dbt Semantic Layer is now powered by MetricFlow, following our acquisition of Transform in 2023. We're excited to share more details about the product and how data teams and the business stakeholders they serve are using the dbt Semantic Layer to get consistent metrics wherever they work. Check out this quick video and keep reading on to learn more. Tap to unmute Your browser can't play this video. Learn more An error occurred. Try watching this video on www.youtube.com, or enable JavaScript if it is disabled in your browser. This post dives into the top five customer benefits of the dbt Semantic Layer, with pointed examples and technical details to help you get started. Don't miss our upcoming webinar on January 17, 2024 all about how to get value from the dbt Semantic Layer! Bring consistency to your metrics The ability to make effective data-driven decisions is a key driver of competitive advantage. Full stop. To really make the most out of data, organizations need to have a consistent, unified, and understandable view of it. And importantly, that view needs to be easily accessible to both technical and non-technical stakeholders. Teams across a company often use different analytics tools, and when critical metric calculations vary across tools, it's easy to get caught up debating whose version of reality is right. These inconsistencies ultimately fuel distrust in data and data teams, and lead to inefficient decision making due to technical teams needing to be involved in every data question, which further stunts strategic initiatives to be more “data-driven.” dbt Labs was founded to abstract away the complexity of modern data systems so organizations could make trusted decisions, faster. The dbt Semantic Layer serves as the translation layer between your business and data teams and optimizes governance and productivity for both teams. Using the dbt Semantic Layer, organizations can codify their metrics in a centralized place and have confidence that they're getting the same number and context around that number wherever they consume data. Meet users where they are The dbt Semantic Layer is deliberately agnostic to where end data consumers get their information, because in practice, organizations consume analytics data from a multitude of tools, and those tools are always changing. In a world where the cloud data platform is the center of gravity, it's backwards and inadvisable to build and maintain multiple sources of truth. Your data platform can and should do the heavy lifting: define metrics centrally and then query and access them in whatever tool suits a particular team. The dbt Semantic Layer offers integrations with a number of data platforms and analytics tools, and we're investing to continually grow our ecosystem. The dbt Semantic Layer meets developers where they are, as well. We support generic interfaces like JDBC and GraphQL so developers can build first-class integrations into existing tools, or develop their own homegrown tool. JDBC is a standard connection for databases, and our API is built upon a protocol called Apache ArrowFlight SQL. ArrowFlight SQL is a new protocol that is known for its scalability; the design offers lower latency and reduces processing requirements, even as the amount of data being transferred gets larger. Our JDBC interface for the dbt Semantic Layer provides a mechanism to query metrics and dimensions in a SQL-like manner. This is not only used by our downstream analytics partners to build dbt Semantic Layer integrations, but can also be used in tools that support our driver and allow writing custom SQL (e.g., Hex, Mode, and common IDE tools like DataGrip and DBeaver). Below is an example of using our JDBC interface from our Hex integration. This interface can be used to talk directly to your data platform, too. If you are doing exploratory analysis, you can also join to data that isn't defined in the dbt Semantic Layer in the same request. Our GraphQL interface offers similar functionality as far as querying metrics and dimensions, but is a powerful choice for developers who prefer a more structured language and the flexibility of a strongly-typed interface. Below is an example of querying the same metrics and dimensions from our GraphQL schema explorer. You can check out our GraphQL schema explorer here if you want to play around and test your own queries! All in all, our philosophy is that everyone should use the tool they're most comfortable in to get the answers they need, so our approach is to support a broad scope of interfaces and integrations. Empower downstream stakeholders to get their own answers Your stakeholders—whether a data analyst, product manager, or RevOps leader—want to be able to answer their own questions and investigate datasets from a variety of angles. Without a universal semantic layer, this data lives across a lot of disparate tables and your stakeholders might struggle to turn that data into actionable insights. A few common roadblocks that emerge: They don't know where to find the data they need They aren't comfortable writing SQL or aren't empowered to shape or analyze data They unknowingly aggregate fields incorrectly They unknowingly use the wrong tables or fields to calculate a metric The dbt Semantic Layer mitigates these concerns by:
Providing definitions and other context about a metric as a key part of the development process. We strongly believe that metrics are more than just the number delivered, and that providing metadata around the metrics in code is key to building confidence for end users who are analyzing those numbers. Here's an example definition of a revenue metric, which includes a useful description that would preempt any debates that might emerge about “What do we mean by 'revenue'?”: Another added benefit to defining metrics centrally in dbt Cloud is that you can layer on additional context via the information that comes from the full dbt ecosystem. dbt Explorer shows details about your metrics that you've defined in the dbt Semantic Layer, but also upstream models and sources, providing you rich context to improve your understanding of the data that went into creating downstream metrics. The dbt Explorer image below shows metrics defined in the dbt Semantic Layer as yellow nodes in the lineage graph. You can click each node to drill further into contextual details and also zoom out to see lineage and dependencies from the entire DAG.
Codifying the aggregation type and underlying calculation. Connecting an analytics tool to tables in the data platform offers users the flexibility to aggregate data as they choose (e.g., via SUM, MIN, MAX, etc.). However, without proper governance, this "self-service" approach may lead to errors in someone's work. This is especially true when dealing with non-additive measures, like those calculated using count distinct or average . The solution to this issue is to define aggregations in code—as done by the dbt Semantic Layer— which allows users to explore freely without compromising data integrity. This ensures that the context of aggregations is sacred when using downstream tools like Tableau, preventing any incorrect analysis. Here is an example of a measure definition that can be used to build your metrics: The dbt Semantic Layer not only captures the underlying measure aggregation but also stores the metric calculation in code, and this is at the heart of how we achieve consistency. For instance, a critical revenue metric derived from the expression revenue - cost is defined explicitly. This prevents users from creating calculations that deviate from the organization's canonical definition of "revenue."
Dynamically rendering relevant dimensions and metrics. Using MetricFlow's join navigation, we can expose not only all of the metrics and dimensions available to query in the scope of an organization's data, but we can also ensure that users are only seeing relevant dimensions when they make a metric selection (and similarly, only see relevant metrics when they make a dimension selection). For example, in our Google Sheets integration, let's say you select a metric called Orders . When you open the list of dimensions available, you'll see a few dimensions related to customer location and order type. You can be confident that these are the dimensions that are applicable to the metric that you selected. If you add a new metric, let's say Revenue , the list of dimensions you can join onto dynamically changes and narrows down to only those applicable for the selected metrics, as you can see in the next image, and we will not show the dimensions that aren't relevant (in this case, the Location dimensions no longer apply once we added Revenue ). These dynamic interactions give the end user confidence that all the dimensions that they see are applicable to their selected metrics, while still providing flexibility to traverse the semantic graph. Optimize direct and indirect costs In the absence of a universal semantic layer, data teams will often build multiple massive cubes of data. These cubes include different time granularities and include many dimensions across a wide dataset. This is both time consuming (rebuilding cubes if changes are required is cumbersome) and expensive (continually building and rebuilding massive cubes with data that may or may not be used drives up consumption). The dbt Semantic Layer solves this problem: with powerful join navigation across data stored in the data platform, you don't have to worry about everything being on one massive table or view as you make metric requests. Consider two semantic models which contain product data and order data, respectively. We can use entities in these models to define how these tables should be joined. As you can see, this order_item semantic model has a couple of foreign key references, and notably we see product is one of them. In this case, we're showing just the entities in the semantic model. Below, we have the products semantic model with a product primary key. With this configuration alone, if a user wants to query any metric, say order_cost (which is built off the order item semantic model) and join it into a dimension from the product semantic model shown below (say product_type ), MetricFlow generates SQL to compute that data dynamically, so you don't need to predict every possible metric and dimension combination that end users might query. An added benefit is that the SQL that MetricFlow generates is optimized for your data platform. It's also legible and we can return it to you if requested. All of our APIs have a compile parameter, which returns the raw SQL MetricFlow generated so you can version control, investigate, and ultimately build trust in the data we return. This is the compile parameter running in Hex, and the output of the full SQL (which includes a join)!
Finally, let's say an end user wants to add a dimension into an existing cube that's used in a downstream tool. The standard workflow requires you to drop the table, create the query to join the data, rebuild the table, and either rename the table or create a new table to hook into downstream tools. With the dbt Semantic Layer, all you have to do is add the dimension into the MetricFlow spec, and it's available to your users. Here's an example of both a categorical ( customer_type ) and time dimension ( first_ordered_at ) definition in the MetricFlow spec. As you can see in both cases, it's only a few lines of code.
We're excited about the potential to optimize spend, and also expect that further cost savings will be realized once we introduce caching in the dbt Semantic Layer (an exciting feature on our roadmap for this year!). Centralize and simplify your code The dbt Semantic Layer improves the “ DRY”-ness of your code as well as the manageability of it. Rather than maintaining metric logic in multiple places, you can put it in one place and it is a consistent interface to your data since it's colocated with your dbt models. This also makes it very easy to future-proof your data access. As you onboard new tools, you don't need to rewrite any logic—it's all already in the dbt Semantic Layer. Additionally, our metric definition interfaces can help you simplify your code. We offer simple abstractions to create complex metrics, rather than requiring you to write a bunch of hairy SQL. For example, take a cumulative metric like cumulative revenue . This seems simple, but the SQL to calculate it can be quite messy. Here's an example of how this can be made simpler—just six lines, including metadata—in MetricFlow: Compare that to the underlying SQL required to actually calculate it, which requires over three times the amount of code, and it still doesn't include any contextual information. Let MetricFlow do the heavy-lifting for you with our simple interfaces! Getting started The dbt Semantic Layer was built to address real issues—in data trust, data velocity, and data costs—that hold organizations back from embracing a truly data-driven culture. By defining semantic objects on top of dbt models and letting MetricFlow do the heavy lifting of generating the SQL, data teams have a scalable, cost-effective way to ensure metric consistency. And through our simple consumption interfaces, we have a large and growing ecosystem of analytics partners building integrations into the dbt Semantic Layer so that business stakeholders are empowered to get answers in whatever tool they're most comfortable in. To get started building out your dbt Semantic Layer configurations, check out our documentation, and reach out in Community Slack (#dbt-cloud-semantic-layer) if you have any questions! Stay tuned for a Part 2 of this blog post that will provide a deeper technical dive into all things dbt Semantic Layer. In the meantime, be sure to join us January 17 for a webinar where we'll walk through core concepts and do a live demo of the dbt Semantic Layer! This information might be about you, your preferences, or your device, and is mostly used to make the site work as you expect. The information does not usually identify you directly, but it can give you a more personalized web experience. Because we respect your right to privacy, you can choose not to allow the processing and use of some types of cookies. Click on the different category headings to learn more and change our default settings. Blocking some types of cookies may impact your experience of the site and the services we are able to offer. More Information Allow All Manage Consent Preferences Strictly Necessary Cookies Always Active Strictly necessary cookies are necessary for the site to function properly and cannot be switched off in our systems. These cookies are usually only set in response to actions made by you that amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in, or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but blocking these cookies will prevent the site from functioning properly. These cookies typically do not store personal data. Functional Cookies Always Active Functional cookies enable our sites to provide enhanced functionality and personalization. They may be set by us or by third party service providers whose services we have added to our sites. If you reject these cookies, then some or all of these services may not function properly. Performance Cookies Always Active Performance cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our sites. These cookies help us understand how our sites are being used, such as which sites are the most and least popular and how people navigate around the sites. The information collected in these cookies are aggregated, meaning that the do not relate to you personally. Opting out of these cookies will prevent us from knowing when you have visited our site and will prevent us from monitoring site performance. In some cases, these cookies may be sent to our third party service providers to help us manage these analytics. Targeting Cookies Always Active Targeting cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners. They may be used by those companies to build a profile of your interests and show you relevant advertisements on other sites. These cookies do not store directly personal information, but are based on uniquely identifying your browser and device. If you do not allow these cookies, you will experience less targeted advertising. Layer with dbt MetricFlow Voiced by Amazon Polly By Hitesh Verma May 21, 2026 Table of Contents Overview Introduction dbt MetricFlow Importance of MetricFlow Key Concepts in MetricFlow Best Practices Common Challenges and Considerations Real-World Use Case The Future of Data Modeling Conclusion Voiced by Amazon Polly Overview In modern data ecosystems, organizations often struggle with a familiar problem: different teams define the same metrics differently. Revenue, active users, churn, these seemingly simple concepts can vary across dashboards, leading to confusion and mistrust in data. This is where a semantic layer becomes essential, and tools like dbt and its MetricFlow engine are transforming how teams define, manage, and consume metrics. Pioneers in Cloud Consulting & Migration Services Reduced infrastructural costs Accelerated application deployment Get Started Introduction A semantic layer is an abstraction layer that sits between raw data and business users. It standardizes definitions of key metrics and dimensions, ensuring consistency across all downstream tools such as BI dashboards, reports, and data applications. Instead of each analyst writing their own SQL to calculate metrics, the semantic layer provides a centralized, reusable definition. This improves data governance, reduces duplication, and ensures that everyone is working with the same version of the truth. dbt MetricFlow MetricFlow is a powerful component within the dbt ecosystem that enables you to define metrics declaratively and query them dynamically. It builds on dbt's transformation capabilities by adding a structured way to define metrics, dimensions, and relationships. At its core, MetricFlow allows you to: Define metrics once using YAML Automatically generate optimized SQL queries Query metrics consistently across tools Handle joins, aggregations, and time grains seamlessly This approach shifts the focus from writing SQL repeatedly to defining business logic once and reusing it everywhere. Importance of MetricFlow Before diving into implementation, it's important to understand the benefits: Consistency Across Teams – Metric definitions are centralized, eliminating discrepancies between dashboards. Faster Development – Analysts don't need to rewrite complex SQL queries for common metrics. Improved Data Governance – Metrics are version-controlled and tested within dbt. Flexibility and Scalability – MetricFlow dynamically builds queries based on user requests, supporting multiple dimensions and filters. Key Concepts in MetricFlow To build a semantic layer using MetricFlow, you need to understand a few core components: Models – These are dbt models that represent transformed data tables. They serve as the foundation for metrics. Semantic Models – Semantic models define how data should be interpreted. They include: Entities (e.g., user_id, order_id) Dimensions (e.g., date, region) Measures (e.g., revenue, count) Metrics – Metrics are business – level aggregations built on top of measures. For example: Total Revenue Daily Active Users Conversion Rate Dimensions and Time Grains – MetricFlow allows slicing metrics by dimensions like geography or time (day, week, month). Best Practices To build an effective semantic layer, consider the following: Keep Models Clean and Modular – Ensure your dbt models are well-structured and follow naming conventions. Use Clear Naming for Metrics – Metric names should be intuitive and aligned with business terminology. Document Everything – Use dbt's documentation features to describe metrics and dimensions. Test Your Metrics – Implement tests to validate accuracy and consistency. Collaborate with Stakeholders – Work closely with business teams to define metrics correctly. Common Challenges and Considerations While MetricFlow is powerful, there are some challenges: Initial Setup Complexity: Defining semantic models requires careful planning. Learning Curve: Teams need to adapt to a declarative approach. Performance Considerations: Complex queries may need optimization. However, these challenges are outweighed by the long-term benefits of consistency and scalability. Real-World Use Case Imagine an e-commerce company where marketing, finance, and product teams all track revenue differently. By implementing a semantic layer with MetricFlow: Revenue is defined once All dashboards use the same definition Teams can slice data by region, time, or product without rewriting SQL This leads to faster decision-making and increased trust in data. The Future of Data Modeling The shift toward semantic layers reflects a broader trend in data engineering: moving from raw data manipulation to business-centric modeling. Tools like dbt and MetricFlow are bridging the gap between data engineers and business users. As organizations scale, the need for consistent, reliable metrics becomes critical. Conclusion Building a semantic layer with dbt MetricFlow is a game-changer for modern data teams. It centralizes metric definitions, improves consistency, and accelerates analytics workflows. While the initial investment in setting up semantic models may seem significant, the long-term payoff in terms of trust, efficiency, and scalability is immense. If your organization is struggling with inconsistent metrics or duplicated logic, adopting a semantic layer powered by dbt MetricFlow could be the solution you need. Drop a query if you have any questions regarding dbt MetricFlow, and we will get back to you quickly. Empowering organizations to become 'data driven' enterprises with our Cloud experts. CloudThat specializes in Cloud Migration, Data Platforms, DevOps, Security, IoT, and advanced technologies like Gen AI & AI/ML. It has delivered over 750 consulting projects for 850+ organizations in 30+ countries as it continues to empower professionals and enterprises to thrive in the digital-first world. FAQs
What is a semantic layer in data analytics? ANS: – A semantic layer is an abstraction layer that standardizes how business metrics and dimensions are defined and accessed. It ensures consistency by providing a single source of truth for metrics across all reporting and analytics tools.
How does dbt MetricFlow differ from traditional SQL-based reporting? ANS: – MetricFlow allows you to define metrics declaratively instead of writing SQL repeatedly. It automatically generates optimized SQL queries, reducing duplication and ensuring consistent metric definitions across teams.
What are the main components of MetricFlow? ANS: – The key components include semantic models, entities, dimensions, measures, and metrics. These elements work together to define how data is structured and how business logic is applied. Source: Checkpoint - Great Expectations Documentation
URL: https://docs.greatexpectations.io/docs/0.18/reference/learn/terms/checkpoint
Checkpoint | Great Expectations Skip to main content Get started with GX Cloud by joining our bi-weekly hands on workshop. 0.18.21 1.16.1 0.18.21 0.17.x and earlier Search 12k 1.8k Try GX Cloud Home GX Cloud GX Core Learn GX API Expectations gallery Help Get support Compatibility reference Community Request a demo Versions 1.16.1 0.18.21 0.17.x and earlier 12k 1.8k Home GX Cloud GX Core Learn GX API Expectations gallery Help Try GX Cloud ← Back to main menu GX V0 to V1 Migration Guide Expectation classes MetricProviders Usage statistics Glossary Action Batch Batch Request Custom Expectation Checkpoint Data Source Data Context Data Asset Data Assistant Data Docs Evaluation Parameter Execution Engine Expectations Expectation Suite Metric Stores Renderer Supporting Resources Validator Validation Result This is documentation for Great Expectations 0.18.21, which is no longer actively maintained. For up-to-date documentation, see the latest version ( 1.16.1). Glossary Checkpoint Version: 0.18.21 On this page Checkpoint A Checkpoint is the primary means for validating data in a production deployment of Great Expectations. Checkpoints provide a convenient abstraction for bundling the Validation The act of applying an Expectation Suite to a Batch. of a Batch (or Batches) A selection of records from a Data Asset. of data against an Expectation Suite A collection of verifiable assertions about data. (or several), as well as the Actions A Python class with a run method that takes a Validation Result and does something with it that should be taken after the validation. Like Expectation Suites and Validation Results Generated when data is Validated against an Expectation or Expectation Suite., Checkpoints are managed using a Data Context The primary entry point for a Great Expectations deployment, with configurations and methods for all supporting components., and have their own Store which is used to persist their configurations to YAML files. These configurations can be committed to version control and shared with your team. Relationships to other objects A Checkpoint uses a Validator Used to run an Expectation Suite against data. to run one or more Expectation Suites against one or more Batches provided by one or more Batch Requests Provided to a Data Source in order to create a Batch.. Running a Checkpoint produces Validation Results and will result in optional Actions being performed if they are configured to do so. Use cases In the Validate Data step of working with Great Expectations, there are two points in which you will interact with Checkpoints in different ways: First, when you create them. And secondly, when you use them to actually Validate your data. Reusable You do not need to re-create a Checkpoint every time you Validate data. If you have created a Checkpoint that covers your data Validation needs, you can save and re-use it for your future Validation needs. Since you can set Checkpoints up to receive some of their required information (like Batch Requests) at run time, it is easy to create Checkpoints that can be readily applied to multiple disparate sources of data. Actions One of the most powerful features of Checkpoints is that they can be configured to run Actions, which will do some process based on the Validation Results generated when a Checkpoint is run. Typical uses include sending email, slack, or custom notifications. Another common use case is updating Data Docs sites. However, Actions can be created to do anything you are capable of programing in Python. This gives you an incredibly versatile tool for integrating Checkpoints in your pipeline's workflow! To set up common Action use cases, see Configure Actions. The classes that implement Checkpoints are in the great_expectations.checkpoint module. Create Creating a Checkpoint is part of the initial setup for data validation. Checkpoints are reusable and only need to be created once, although you can create multiple Checkpoints to cover multiple Validation use cases. For more information about creating Checkpoints, see How to create a new Checkpoint. After you create a Checkpoint, you can use it to Validate data by running it against a Batch or Batches of data. The Batch Requests used by a Checkpoint during this process may be pre-defined and saved as part of the Checkpoint's configuration, or the Checkpoint can be configured to accept one or more Batch Request at run time. For more information about data validation, see How to validate data by running a Checkpoint. In its most basic form, a Checkpoint accepts an expectation_suite_name identfying the test suite to run, and a batch_request identifying the data to test. Checkpoint can be directly directly in Python as follows: Python For an in-depth guide on Checkpoint creation, see our guide on how to create a new Checkpoint. Configure A Checkpoint uses its configuration to determine what data to Validate against which Expectation Suite(s), and what actions to perform on the Validation Results - these validations and Actions are executed by calling a Checkpoint's run method (analogous to calling validate with a single Batch). Checkpoint configurations are very flexible. At one end of the spectrum, you can specify a complete configuration in a Checkpoint's YAML file, and simply call my_checkpoint.run() . At the other end, you can specify a minimal configuration in the YAML file and provide missing keys as kwargs when calling run . At runtime, a Checkpoint configuration has three required and three optional keys, and is built using a combination of the YAML configuration and any kwargs passed in at runtime: Required keys name : user-selected Checkpoint name (e.g. "staging_tables") config_version : version number of the Checkpoint configuration validations : a list of dictionaries that describe each validation that is to be executed, including any actions. Each validation dictionary has three required and three optional keys: Required keys batch_request : a dictionary describing the batch of data to validate (learn more about specifying Batches here: Batches) expectation_suite_name : the name of the Expectation Suite to validate the batch of data against action_list : a list of actions to perform after each batch is validated Optional keys name : providing a name will allow referencing the validation inside the run by name (e.g. " user_table_validation") evaluation_parameters : used to define named parameters using Great Expectations Evaluation Parameter syntax runtime_configuration : provided to the Validator's runtime_configuration (e.g. result_format ) Optional keys class_name : the class of the Checkpoint to be instantiated, defaults to Checkpoint template_name : the name of another Checkpoint to use as a base template run_name_template : a template to create run names, using environment variables and datetime-template syntax (e.g. "%Y-%M-staging-$MY_ENV_VAR") Configure defaults and parameter override behavior Checkpoint configurations follow a nested pattern, where more general keys provide defaults for more specific ones. For instance, any required validation dictionary keys (e.g. expectation_suite_name ) can be specified at the top-level (i.e. at the same level as the validations list), serving as runtime defaults. Starting at the earliest reference template, if a configuration key is re-specified, its value can be appended, updated, replaced, or cause an error when redefined. Replaced name module_name class_name run_name_template expectation_suite_name Updated batch_request : at runtime, if a key is re-defined, an error will be thrown action_list : actions that share the same user-defined name will be updated, otherwise a new action will be appended evaluation_parameters runtime_configuration Appended action_list : actions that share the same user-defined name will be updated, otherwise a new action will be appended validations Checkpoint configuration default and override behavior No nesting Nesting with defaults Keys passed at runtime Using template This configuration specifies full validation dictionaries - no nesting (defaults) are used. When run, this Checkpoint will perform one validation of a single batch of data, against a single Expectation Suite ("my_expectation_suite"). YAML: YAML runtime: Python This configuration specifies four top-level keys ("expectation_suite_name", "action_list", "evaluation_parameters", and "runtime_configuration") that can serve as defaults for each validation, allowing the keys to be omitted from the validation dictionaries. When run, this Checkpoint will perform two Validations of two different Batches of data, both against the same Expectation Suite ("my_expectation_suite"). Each Validation will trigger the same set of Actions and use the same Evaluation Parameters A dynamic value used during Validation of an Expectation which is populated by evaluating simple expressions or by referencing previously generated metrics. and runtime configuration. YAML: YAML Runtime: Python Results: Python This configuration omits the "validations" key from the YAML, which means a "validations " list must be provided when the Checkpoint is run. Because "action_list", "evaluation_parameters", and "runtime_configuration" appear as top-level keys in the YAML configuration, these keys may be omitted from the validation dictionaries, unless a non-default value is desired. When run, this Checkpoint will perform two validations of two different batches of data, with each batch of data validated against a different Expectation Suite ("my_expectation_suite" and "my_other_expectation_suite", respectively). Each Validation will trigger the same set of actions and use the same Evaluation Parameters A dynamic value used during Validation of an Expectation which is populated by evaluating simple expressions or by referencing previously generated metrics. and runtime configuration. YAML: YAML Runtime: Python Results: Python This configuration references the Checkpoint detailed in the previous example ("Keys passed at runtime"), allowing the runtime call to be much slimmer. YAML: YAML Runtime: Python Results: Python CheckpointResult The return object of a Checkpoint run is a CheckpointResult object. The run_results attribute forms the backbone of this type and defines the basic contract for what a Checkpoint's run method returns. It is a dictionary where the top-level keys are the ValidationResultIdentifiers of the Validation Results generated in the run. Each value is a dictionary having at minimum, a validation_result key containing an ExpectationSuiteValidationResult and an actions_results key containing a dictionary where the top-level keys are names of Actions performed after that particular Validation, with values containing any relevant outputs of that action (at minimum and in many cases, this would just be a dictionary with the Action's class_name ). The run_results dictionary can contain other keys that are relevant for a specific Checkpoint implementation. For example, the run_results dictionary from a WarningAndFailureExpectationSuiteCheckpoint might have an extra key named "expectation_suite_severity_level" to indicate if the suite is at either a "warning" or "failure" level. CheckpointResult objects include many convenience methods (e.g. list_data_asset_names ) that make working with Checkpoint results easier. You can learn more about these methods in the documentation for class: great_expectations.checkpoint.types.checkpoint_result.CheckpointResult . Below is an example of a CheckpointResult object which itself contains ValidationResult , ExpectationSuiteValidationResult , and CheckpointConfig objects. Example CheckpointResult Python Example script To view the full script used in this page, see checkpoints_and_actions.py Was this topic helpful? Checkpoints provide a convenient abstraction for running a number of validation definitions and triggering a set of actions to be taken after the validation step. Source: Checkpoints and Actions | Great Expectations
URL: https://legacy.017.docs.greatexpectations.io/docs/0.14.13/reference/checkpoints_and_actions/
Checkpoints and Actions | Great Expectations Skip to main content 0.14.13 0.17.23 0.16.16 0.15.50 0.14.13 Search K Product GX CLOUD GX OSS Community COMMUNITY HOME COMMUNITY FORUM GITHUB JOIN THE EMAIL LIST RESOURCES INTEGRATIONS DOCUMENTATION EXPECTATION GALLERY GREAT EXPECTATIONS BLOG GREAT EXPECTATIONS CASE STUDIES Company ABOUT US CAREERS GX Cloud Versions 0.17.23 0.16.16 0.15.50 0.14.13 Product Community RESOURCES Company GX Cloud ← Back to main menu This is documentation for Great Expectations 0.14.13, which is no longer actively maintained. For up-to-date documentation, see the latest version. Version: 0.14.13 On this page Checkpoints and Actions Introduction API note As part of the new modular expectations API in Great Expectations, Validation Operators are evolving into Checkpoints. At some point in the future Validation Operators will be fully deprecated. The batch.validate() method evaluates one Batch of data against one Expectation Suite and returns a dictionary of Validation Results. This is sufficient when you explore your data and get to know Great Expectations. When deploying Great Expectations in a real data pipeline, you will typically discover additional needs: Validating a group of Batches that are logically related (for example, a Checkpoint for all staging tables). Validating a Batch against several Expectation Suites (for example, run three suites to protect a machine learning model churn.critical , churn.warning , churn.drift ). Doing something with the Validation Results (for example, saving them for later review, sending notifications in case of failures, etc.). Checkpoints provide a convenient abstraction for bundling the validation of a Batch (or Batches) of data against an Expectation Suite (or several), as well as the actions that should be taken after the validation. Like Expectation Suites and Validation Results, Checkpoints are managed using a Data Context, and have their own Store which is used to persist their configurations to YAML files. These configurations can be committed to version control and shared with your team. The classes that implement Checkpoints are in the great_expectations.checkpoint module. Validation Actions Actions are Python classes with a run method that takes the result of validating a Batch against an Expectation Suite and does something with it (e.g., save Validation Results to disk, or send a Slack notification). Classes that implement this API can be configured to be added to the list of actions used by a particular Checkpoint. Classes that implement Actions can be found in the great_expectations.checkpoint.actions module. Checkpoint configuration A Checkpoint uses its configuration to determine what data to validate against which Expectation Suite(s), and what actions to perform on the Validation Results - these validations and actions are executed by calling a Checkpoint's run method (analogous to calling validate with a single Batch). Checkpoint configurations are very flexible. At one end of the spectrum, you can specify a complete configuration in a Checkpoint's YAML file, and simply call my_checkpoint.run() . At the other end, you can specify a minimal configuration in the YAML file and provide missing keys as kwargs when calling run . At runtime, a Checkpoint configuration has three required and three optional keys, and is built using a combination of the YAML configuration and any kwargs passed in at runtime: Required keys name : user-selected Checkpoint name (e.g. "staging_tables") config_version : version number of the Checkpoint configuration validations : a list of dictionaries that describe each validation that is to be executed, including any actions. Each validation dictionary has three required and three optional keys: Required keys Optional keys Optional keys class_name : the class of the Checkpoint to be instantiated, defaults to Checkpoint template_name : the name of another Checkpoint to use as a base template run_name_template : a template to create run names, using environment variables and datetime-template syntax (e.g. " %Y-%M-staging-$MY_ENV_VAR") Configuration defaults and parameter override behavior Checkpoint configurations follow a nested pattern, where more general keys provide defaults for more specific ones. For instance, any required validation dictionary keys (e.g. expectation_suite_name ) can be specified at the top-level ( i.e. at the same level as the validations list), serving as runtime defaults. Starting at the earliest reference template, if a configuration key is re-specified, its value can be appended, updated, replaced, or cause an error when redefined. Replaced name module_name class_name run_name_template expectation_suite_name Updated batch_request : at runtime, if a key is re-defined, an error will be thrown action_list : actions that share the same user-defined name will be updated, otherwise a new action will be appended evaluation_parameters runtime_configuration Appended action_list : actions that share the same user-defined name will be updated, otherwise a new action will be appended validations API note If the use case calls for instantiating the Checkpoint explicitly, then it is crucial to ensure that only serializable values are passed as arguments to the constructor. Specifically, if batch_request is specified at any level of the hierarchy of the Checkpoint configuration (at the top level and/or as part of the validators list structure), then no runtime batch_request can contain batch_data , only a database query. This is because batch_data is used to specify dataframes (Pandas, Spark), which are not serializable (while database queries are plain text, which is serializable). The proper mechanism for specifying non-serializable parameters is to pass them dynamically to the Checkpoint run() method. Hence, in a typical scenario, one would instantiate the Checkpoint class with serializable parameters only, while specifying any non-serializable parameters, commonly dataframes, as arguments to the Checkpoint run() method. SimpleCheckpoint class For many use cases, the SimpleCheckpoint class can be used to simplify the process of specifying a Checkpoint configuration. SimpleCheckpoint provides a basic set of actions - store Validation Result, store evaluation parameters, update Data Docs, and optionally, send a Slack notification - allowing you to omit an action_list from your configuration and at runtime. Configurations using the SimpleCheckpoint class can optionally specify four additional top-level keys that customize and extend the basic set of default actions: site_names : a list of Data Docs site names to update as part of the update Data Docs action - defaults to "all" slack_webhook : if provided, an action will be added that sends a Slack notification to the provided webhook notify_on : used to define when a notification is fired, according to Validation Result outcome - all , failure , or success . Defaults to all . notify_with : a list of Data Docs site names for which to include a URL in any notifications - defaults to all CheckpointResult The return object of a Checkpoint run is a CheckpointResult object. The run_results attribute forms the backbone of this type and defines the basic contract for what a Checkpoint's run method returns. It is a dictionary where the top-level keys are the ValidationResultIdentifiers of the Validation Results generated in the run. Each value is a dictionary having at minimum, a validation_result key containing an ExpectationSuiteValidationResult and an actions_results key containing a dictionary where the top-level keys are names of actions performed after that particular validation, with values containing any relevant outputs of that action (at minimum and in many cases, this would just be a dictionary with the action's class_name ). The run_results dictionary can contain other keys that are relevant for a specific Checkpoint implementation. For example, the run_results dictionary from a WarningAndFailureExpectationSuiteCheckpoint might have an extra key named " expectation_suite_severity_level" to indicate if the suite is at either a "warning" or "failure" level. CheckpointResult objects include many convenience methods (e.g. list_data_asset_names ) that make working with Checkpoint results easier. You can learn more about these methods in the documentation for class: great_expectations.checkpoint.types.checkpoint_result.CheckpointResult . Below is an example of a CheckpointResult object which itself contains ValidationResult , ExpectationSuiteValidationResult , and CheckpointConfig objects. Example CheckpointResult: Checkpoint configuration default and override behavior No nesting Nesting with defaults Keys passed at runtime Using template Using SimpleCheckpoint This configuration specifies full validation dictionaries - no nesting (defaults) are used. When run, this Checkpoint will perform one validation of a single batch of data, against a single Expectation Suite ("my_expectation_suite"). YAML: runtime: This configuration specifies four top-level keys ("expectation_suite_name", "action_list", "evaluation_parameters", and "runtime_configuration") that can serve as defaults for each validation, allowing the keys to be omitted from the validation dictionaries. When run, this Checkpoint will perform two validations of two different batches of data, both against the same Expectation Suite ("my_expectation_suite"). Each validation will trigger the same set of actions and use the same evaluation parameters and runtime configuration. YAML: Runtime: Results: This configuration omits the "validations" key from the YAML, which means a "validations " list must be provided when the Checkpoint is run. Because "action_list", "evaluation_parameters", and "runtime_configuration" appear as top-level keys in the YAML configuration, these keys may be omitted from the validation dictionaries, unless a non-default value is desired. When run, this Checkpoint will perform two validations of two different batches of data, with each batch of data validated against a different Expectation Suite ("my_expectation_suite" and "my_other_expectation_suite", respectively). Each validation will trigger the same set of actions and use the same evaluation parameters and runtime configuration. YAML: Runtime: Results: This configuration references the Checkpoint detailed in the previous example ("Keys passed at runtime"), allowing the runtime call to be much slimmer. YAML: Runtime: Results: This configuration specifies the SimpleCheckpoint class under the "class_name" key, allowing for a much slimmer configuration.
